Jeme A Brelin (jeme at brelin.net) typed this ...
> On Sun, 17 Feb 2008, Rich Shepard wrote:
> >   The goal is to pass inspection without raising any eyebrows and 
> > bringing the computer back home again.
> 
> Oh, come on.

No, that's really the goal.

> Since the searches themselves are totally meritless and irrational, why 
> would you think you could come up with some rational way to second-guess 
> the inspectors?

Well, even though it would make me want to gag (and other expressions of
extreme distaste), being able to conidently log someone into my laptop
and demonstrate that there is absolutely nothing suspicious without
giving away any confidential information is a good start. 

Being a white male and travelling with my wife and daughter will
probably also help.

Telling the customs agents how ridiculous and unfair the search is will
NOT help, nor will demanding my rights as a US citizen, because as far
as they're concerned, I have none until they allow me past the customs
gate. I happen to think that's as wrong as what the TSA is doing, but
this is apparently an old US Customs policy.

http://www.schneier.com/blog/archives/2008/02/us_customs_seiz.html

> If you wanted to smuggle data into the US, you would just use SSH and ship 
> it across the internet to a machine somewhere.  Anyone who wishes to 
> actually do such a thing has already done so.

And of course I do archive my changes over a secure connection to a
server that I control at every opportunity. That's not the issue. The
issue is getting home with my laptop still in my posession.

> My personal belief is that the laptop confiscation scare exists to 
> discourage overseas travel, create an illusion of threat, and possibly 
> make bloggers think twice about checking out the world situation 
> first-hand.

Personally, I think the 'scare' exists to sell whatever media the scare-
author-du-jour gets his paycheck from. But I'd rather be safe than
sorry.

> Put your laptop in a DHL box and ship it home.  Mark the customs label 
> "office equipment and documents" and be done with it.

Would you ship your laptop across the country in a box? I wouldn't. So
I'm certainly not going to ship it across the ocean and then across the
country. 

My un-scientific guess is that confiscation is fairly rare, and has
always been fairly rare. This is not a new regulation or policy, as far
as I can tell.  I have travelled outside the US with a laptop in the
past, and have never even felt like there was a threat that my laptop
would be confiscated. But in these (gag!) post 9/11 times, I want to be
as sure as I can be that my laptop will arrive home at exactly the same
time that I do.

--Kurt

On Feb 17, 2008 6:29 PM, Kristian Erik Hermansen
<kristian.hermansen at gmail.com> wrote:
> On Feb 17, 2008 4:21 PM, Kurt Sussman <plug at merlot.com> wrote:
> > I'm looking for a technical solution to a technical problem, not a
>
> How about GUI auto-login to your laptop with virtualbox that starts up
> Windows under Linux :-P

The problem is Grub.  As far as I can tell there is no way to get grub
to be absolutely silent about what it is doing.  Even with
"hiddenmenu" and "timeout 0" in the menu.lst file, grub still blabs
"starting grub ...", if only for a split second.

Now if you could get grub to be quiet, then you could dual boot into
Windows 98/2000 by default.  When you need to do real work, you reboot
and press ESC after the POST screen to bring up the grub menu, select
linux, and away you go.

Regards,
- Robert

From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sun, 17 Feb 2008 19:35:41 -0800

	<20080218001548.GS23444@merlot.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0802171651460.27103@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20080218033541.GM30881@sentinelchicken.org>

> > ... being able to conidently log someone into my laptop and demonstrate
> > that there is absolutely nothing suspicious without giving away any
> > confidential information is a good start.
> 
>    And therein lies the rub: what is "suspicious" to those who hold the keys
> to access the country? I suspect that it is a highly classified secret.


I have pondered this overall issue several times as it very important to
me.  My laptop sometimes contains very sensitive security information
that my clients have provided to me under NDA.  I think the only
solution that keeps me sleeping sound is to encrypt the partition with
the data.  I could just encrypt files individually, but chances are I'd
forget some of them (or to wipe some deleted copy of them).  In your
case, it would be a pain to think about every possible dot file you want
to clean out.  Any third party tool would probably only work for the
specific software it knows about.  Just encrypt /home and don't worry
about it.

While I agree that encrypting a hard drive would look suspicious to
border guards, I believe having it run Linux alone would be sufficiently
suspicious for them and if they had a chip on their shoulder that day,
they'd surely image my drive.  If you really want to try and pull the
wool over on them with a "normal" computer, have a separate Windoze
partition that looks tame. 

For me, the data I carry is worth more than the laptop.  If they take my
laptop while the data is hopelessly inaccessible to them, at least I can
just tell my client: "I need to get that data from you again, but don't
worry, your network isn't compromised because of it."

HTH,
tim

Also, you'd be better off at least 2 channels off of the "normal" ones
(1, 6 and 11).  So, instead of 5, try 3 or 4 or 8 or 9.  iwlist

From russell at personaltelco.net  Sun Feb 17 22:08:57 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 17 Feb 2008 22:08:57 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Metro-fi & Wireless Channels
In-Reply-To: <422ec1510802170036x74871372u786e673eac91b810@mail.gmail.com>
References: <422ec1510802170036x74871372u786e673eac91b810@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86ir0m94p2.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "kurt" == kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> writes:

kurt> Hey guys, I'm trying to set up my wireless network again, and
kurt> both my netgear routers keep failing. My roomate said it's
kurt> probably the wireless channels interfering with each other. When
kurt> I scan for local networks, the only ones that come up are
kurt> Metro-fi Free and Metro-fi Premium. Do you know what channel
kurt> Metro-fi uses? 

MetroFi sticks to the three non-overlapping channels to minimize its
own self-interference.

kurt> Does it matter? 

You don't want to be on the same channel as some high-powered
transmitter that is generating a lot of traffic.

kurt> Could they possibly be interfering with my equipment?

Not intentionally.  But many things are possible.

kurt> I read Netgear's site and it recommended setting channels at
kurt> least 5 channels apart from your neighbors to reduce
kurt> interference. Said also that there are 11 total channels, 1 -
kurt> 11. Which one would you guys recommend using? Any tools you can
kurt> recommend for monitoring channels of local wireless networks?

You can use iwlist <iface> scan, or better is kismet.  Kismet will
show you all the networks it can hear, they can be sorted various ways
(by channel, etc), will give you a sense of which ones are busiest,
etc.

The traditional recommendation is to try to stick to 1, 6 or 11,
because these three channels do not interfere with each other.  Most
off-the-shelf wifi routers default to channel 6.  However, in very
dense wifi environments, you are going to get interference on any of
those channels.  If you are actually seeing interference problems, you
can try 2 channels away from those three: 3 or 4, or 8 or 9 (3 and 9
seem more popular).  Here is a table of network channel choice from
the couple months of my stumble data:

  ntwks ch
------- --
   6655 1
    553 2
    778 3
    546 4
    501 5
  17523 6
    557 7
    667 8
   3613 9
    855 10
   8569 11

Kismet will tell you if anyone nearby is using those already.
Basically, you are looking for maximum separation from whoever is
transmitting loudest and most (a very-loud network that isn't being
used much doesn't interfere as much as a medium-loud network that is
transmitting a lot).


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Sounds wise.  I worry a lot more about the laptop getting taken by
thieves.  Then I have some explaining to do to my clients.  If it
is taken by customs, then my clients still get upset, but not at
me.  If they are a foreign site for a US company, then then we
apply pressure through my congressman and theirs.  If it is a 
big foreign company, the pressure comes via the State Department.  
Either way, I suspect some Customs officer has a lot of explaining
to do.

I always travel with my laptop.  It is a secure platform, and has
all the things I need to do my work.  Reconstructing that
environment on another machine elsewhere would take a week. 
I could also travel with just the clothes on my back, a passport,
and a cash card, and buy all the stuff I need when I get there,
but that would be time consuming, too.  

My laptop gets completely backed up before I leave.  On long trips,
I usually carry along a spare hard drive in a drive bay, and do a
drive image copy twice a week or so.  So to really inconvenience
me, they would have to take both.  In extremis, I can slowly move
data over the net both ways, but that means going into my firewalled
systems from a customer machine I probably shouldn't trust.

The real worry is what they do to foreign nationals entering and
leaving the US.  Later this week, my Dutch friend Jaap will be over
here to do some installations at Intel (and go to Advanced Topics
if he isn't too jetlagged);  his last trip through US customs was
not very pleasant.  This compares to travelling around western
Europe; he can drive into Belgium or Germany without even slowing
down for the "entering Belgium" style signs on the border. 

If we make ourselves too hard to do business with, then we will
suffer badly, technologically.  Jaap works for ASML, which makes
semiconductor steppers (photomask imaging machines).  The other
big stepper maker is Canon.  If both companies find it too much
hassle to do business with the US, then Intel (and AMD, and IBM,
and TI, and Micron ...) can't make chips.  I wonder how long we
would last after that?

Keith

From Jason.VanCleve at chrome.com  Thu Feb 14 15:54:18 2008
From: Jason.VanCleve at chrome.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Thu, 14 Feb 2008 15:54:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Advanced Topics February meeting
In-Reply-To: <20080214114835.e98be59f.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <27528.198.182.194.170.1202863311.squirrel@clueserver.org><1202879883.9267.4.camel@kratos.fnordora.org><20080213072234.GK2426@sentinelchicken.org><00f201c86e71$981ebc10$c85c3430$@net><20080213114601.8740d1b3.johnxj@comcast.net><47B463D5.7080102@cesmail.net>
	<20080214114835.e98be59f.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1DEC803BC73785498C98EF74AA6ECFEA01AC6F6B@pdxmsx01.chrome.dt.inc>

Wow, Access, really?  I wrote a tool for my QA team to read sets of data
into Oracle for analysis, because playing with GUI shapes in Access gets
really frustrating and hard to edit, and its manual SQL
syntax--particular those parentheses-ridden joins--is nigh impossible to
work with.  They love my loader tool, because it gives them a "normal"
SQL interface (not to mention the power of a real RDBMS), and once you
get used to that, you won't go back to using GUIs.

No, there's no such thing as standard SQL, but most systems will support
90% of what you need (Access not included), with only minor differences.
The problem is, with an enterprise app', where performance is critical,
you'll inevitably find yourself tweaking your queries in system-specific
ways:  here a proprietary annotation, there a restructuring of
subqueries, just because your DB likes it better that way.  And that's
where ORMs fall short:  they simply can't know in every case what's the
best way to write a query.  They'll produce something that works, but to
make it hum, you're going to have to write that SQL by hand.

So I recommend biting that bullet and learning the basics of SQL, along
with whatever little quirks your DB introduces.  Learning outer joins,
"group by" and "having", "exists" and "not exists", and a few other
tricks will give you worlds of power, and you'll find those skills
really do transfer from one system to another, including Oracle, Sybase,
MySQL and Postgres (but not Access!).

With that knowledge, you'll be in a good position to tweak your queries
for best performance on whatever system you're using (except, well, you
know).

--Jason

> generators, 
> > etc. Over the past year, I forced myself to learn enough SQL to do 
> > what I need to do with some rather large collections of 
> numeric data 
> > I've saddled myself with over the years, but I find SQL 
> code difficult 
> > to read and difficult to factor, etc.
> > 
> > If you use MySQL, there's a nifty front end called 
> DBDesigner 4 that 
> > helps a lot. But in general, many person decades have gone into web 
> > application frameworks, etc., that present a much saner API 
> than raw SQL.
> 
> One problem I have with SQL is that there seems to be a large 
> variety of flavors of it, all of which are obsessed with 
> having their commas, semicolons, periods, word order, etc. 
> precisely the way they want it.
> Witness the multiple suggestions I received here for how to 
> write a delete query. 
> 
> Then there is the problem that it requires logic. At the 
> moment my logic module is pretty toasted from overexposure to 
> linguistic syntax (algebra for linguists).
> 
> But the biggest problem is the documentation. People who 
> write the documentation assume I know the difference between 
> a "command" and a "keyword," not to mention various other 
> terms. Understanding that stuff is like trying to figure out 
> what your algebra teacher is talking about when you missed 
> the first half of the term.
> 
> Even a GUI like Access, Kexi, OOo Base, et al. is difficult 
> to figure out. But at least you can drag and drop and click 
> on stuff, which automates the writing of the statements. 
> Thus, even a beginner can get the commas, semicolons and 
> periods where they belong. It may not be a lot faster than 
> typing the statements manually, but when you factor in the 
> time and frustration involved in debugging, the GUI is way faster.
> 
> As I said previously, I finally have a solution that works 
> for me. Kexi
> (sqlite3) holds the data and I have no problem editing it, 
> creating new tables, forms, reports and such in Kexi. Its 
> query abilities are GUI also, but very little of sqlite3 has 
> been implemented yet. Basically, the only queries you can do 
> in Kexi at this time are Select queries.
> That is pretty limiting, but OOo rescued me. After much 
> travail I managed to get OOo Base to see the Kexi data. 
> Unfortunately, it sees it in read-only mode, so if I want to 
> edit the data I have to do so in Kexi. And OOo sees only the 
> tables in Kexi - it cannot see the queries, forms and other 
> objects. However, OOo Base has a very nice query engine of 
> its own that is very robust, fully featured, and GUI. You can 
> View the SQL statement in case you want to, but so far I 
> haven't had a need to do so. So now I can edit the data in 
> Kexi and create queries in OOo.
> Ultimately I need the data merged into a Writer document, and 
> OOo will happily merge the Kexi data into a Writer document 
> based on a query in Base. Previously, when I used Access, OOo 
> could merge only on a table, so I had to create a dummy table 
> that I would insert data into, then empty after the merge. 
> Merging on a query is much simpler.
> 
> Theoretically, now that I have OOo Base seeing the Kexi data, 
> I should be able to simply move the data completely into Base 
> and forget about Kexi. But Base requires Java and breaks 
> every time there is a new version of Java. Java is also very 
> slow, and if you read comments from OOo users there is a lot 
> of complaining about speed. I mean serious speed problems. 
> Users report things like two hours to perform an operation in 
> Base that took a few minutes in Kexi. So for the time being 
> I'm just going to leave well enough alone. The version of 
> Base that comes with OOo 2.3 is a relatively new rewrite, and 
> Kexi is also in its infancy. What I have works for me and 
> I'll just bide my time and see how these two develop.

On Mon, 18 Feb 2008 10:05:12 -0800 (PST)
Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> dijo:

> On Mon, 18 Feb 2008, Alan wrote:
> 
> > The Matrox people used to be real good about supporting their products.
> 
>    In the 1980s WordPerfect Corp. (started as Satellite Software
> International) set the standard for customer support. At one time they had
> more than 1,200 support staff working on their Provo campus. (Their campus
> would be the envy of many a college, and their cafeteria served great food
> at very reasonable prices.) No one could touch them. Then they lost their
> way and plummeted out of existence.

WordPerfect set the standard for tech support - free, unlimited 800
numbers. At one time they installed a new telephone system to handle
the incoming calls more efficiently. At 8 am one day they turned it on,
and promptly blew out the incoming 800 service for the whole state of
Utah. WordPerfect is also the source of many apocryphal tech support
stories - e.g., "the cup holder broke." Or my personal favorite:

Tech support: 	"Open and then close the door."
Customer:	"OK." (Sound of footsteps, a door opening, then
closing, more footsteps.)

There was a time when it came to video I said "Matrox or nuthin.'" That
has changed. Today I would say "nVidia or maybe Intel." But my needs
have changed and companies have changed. The only thing that never
changes is that everything is always changing. I still say that not all
companies are run by evil people and open source is not the best
solution 100% of the time. There are too many variables to make blanket
statements.

Kurt Sussman wrote:

> Thanks for all the thoughtful discussion and clever ideas!

I discussed the idea of laptop searches in customs with some friends 
this morning. As someone who knows more than the average person about 
computers, to me it seems pretty easy to hide information from most 
people on a computer if I wanted to. And what kind of idiot would 
physically carry that kind of damning information with them anyway? 
Surely it would be easier to just transfer it electronically through teh 
tubes.

I just installed Truecrypt 5a on a spare laptop with WinXP, to try out 
the system disk encryption. The new bootloader is not encrypted, but 
everything else is. Pretty slick so far, but how would that look to an 
over zealous border guard? I had a web server compromised once, and the 
level of paranoia in me mandated a re-install of the OS. Though the 
evidence of the attack seemed clear that I could clean up the mess and 
that attack wasn't even entirely successful, I couldn't trust it 100%. 
It isn't a lot different with this issue in customs; how far will they 
go to be satisfied?  If I were in the position of being a border guard, 
and these laptops were going through me I know enough to know that I'm 
nowhere near the level needed to properly process the information. In 
fact, I'd bet no one employed in this position with customs currently is 
either.

It seems so futile for them to do this.

I would be interested in hearing how things go when you get back, if any 
suspicion at all arose from your Linux laptop. Probably none, but it's 
crazy enough that this idea got beyond a board meeting at all.

-- 
- m0gely

Yes, odd.  Can you run these commands and post the contents of output.txt:

true ; (
set -x
cat /etc/issue.net
grep -v -e '^#' -e'^$' /boot/grub/menu.lst | cat -A
grub --version
grub-install -v
df -hT /boot/grub/menu.lst
cat /boot/grub/device.map
) &> output.txt

default^I^I0$
timeout^I^I5$
color cyan/blue white/blue$
title^I^IDebian GNU/Linux, kernel 2.6.18-6-486$
root^I^I(hd0,0)$

+ grub --version
-bash: grub: command not found
+ grub-install -v
-bash: grub-install: command not found
+ df -hT /boot/grub/menu.lst
Filesystem    Type    Size  Used Avail Use% Mounted on
/dev/hdb1     ext2    671M  367M  269M  58% /
+ cat /boot/grub/device.map
(hd0)	/dev/hdb

I think you wanted something different for the last two, so:
eehouse at pup:~$ sudo grub --version
grub (GNU GRUB 0.97)
eehouse at pup:~$ sudo grub-install -v
grub-install (GNU GRUB 0.97)

Smells like restraint of trade. Governments are not immune from the
Sherman and Clayton Acts. Oregon has similar laws which are sometimes
more restrictive. A word to the county attorney would probably be
ignored, but is worth thinking about, especially if it comes from your
corporate counsel.

I'm not an expert on anti-trust laws, but Google might turn up
something with IE +antitrust.

Robert Citek wrote:

>> Visiting it with Firefox or Konqueror returns this message:
>>

> 
> Have you tried UserAgentSwitcher for FireFox?
> 
> https://addons.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/addon/59
> 
> It's a kludge.  But if it works, why not?

I tried it (a few minutes ago). While it lets me connect to the site, it
only displays part of what should be there. I suspect they are using
some features if IE that aren't available to other browsers.

That's the problem with the multiple listing service site. It was
designed long enough ago that people were usually connected to it by
dial-up, and they had a real problem with performance. By using some
active-x features they move some of the processing load to the users'
machines and the apparent wait is not as bad. Of course, now that we all
have high speed connections, that's no longer necessary.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Tue Feb 19 15:15:40 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Tue, 19 Feb 2008 15:15:40 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Grub won't launch Debian until I hit return key
In-Reply-To: <20080219225714.GO7237@amethyst.spiritone.com>
References: <mailman.384.1203458113.4375.plug@lists.pdxlinux.org>
	<20080219225714.GO7237@amethyst.spiritone.com>
Message-ID: <20080219231540.GD30881@sentinelchicken.org>

> Is it possible this is related to grub's interaction with the BIOS?  I
> figured by the time grub was displaying the menu it was fully in
> control.

It would be bizarre, but if grub thinks a key had been pressed sometime
after booting, it would certainly no longer boot on a timer.  It's a
stretch, but maybe there's garbage in the keyboard interrupt handler's
buffer left by the BIOS?

I found someone who had a similar issue and their grub would boot
automatically when the timeout was set to 0:

  http://ubuntuforums.org/archive/index.php/t-370462.html

but not when it was set to anything else.  This probably isn't the
solution you're looking for, but might be worth trying to see if it's
got the same symptoms.

tim

On Mon, 18 Feb 2008, Kurt Sussman wrote:
> I did about the same trip last year, and coming in through customs the 
> big issues were "do you have your entry stamp?" and "did you bring any 
> home-packaged meat or cheese from France?". They really didn't care 
> about my laptop, which was hanging on my shoulder as they went over 
> their checklist. We went through customs at LAX and it was really 
> crowded and (to my eye) somewhat disorganized.

After missing my flight out of Barcelona in October, I had to get a 
last-minute ticket out of Amsterdam (and a one-way ticket from Barcelona 
to Amsterdam -- there's a whole story here that I won't repeat).

So I flew into Amsterdam and was there for 12 hours and then flew to 
Atlanta.  The officials at the Amsterdam airport were mildly curious that 
I flew to their city for 12 hours and had purchased my ticket 18 hours 
before and they asked me lots and lots of questions (most of them came 
after the shocking confession that I had *gasp* left the airport (!!) in 
that time).

Upon arriving in Atlanta, however, I went through passport control (no 
questions at all) and then folks were being directed off to the right. 
The woman directing said, "Baggage claim is this way!"  I said, "I have no 
checked bags."  And she looked surprised and said, "Oh... you can go this 
way."  And I walked through a doorway and found myself in one of the 
terminals at ATL.  I don't think I went through customs at all.

Now I wish I *had* done something nefarious in Amsterdam.

J.

> Getting into Canada is a breeze. Canadian border officers tend to be 
> quite polite and efficient.

I used to go to Canada regularly (from BWI) when I was a field analyst 
for FPS. There were some things you couldn't say, like "I'm coming in to 
work." It was OK to say, "Giving a presentation" or "giving a lecture" 
but "coming in to work" was a no-no.

The only really rough time I had was coming in to Ottawa the week Pope 
John Paul II was there. But I got to see the whole thing on TV, and from 
my hotel window, I could see the *back* of the stage where he blessed 
the crowd.

> It's coming back to the US that poses the problem: long lines, 
> prolonged bouts of questioning, and officers with an attitude that 
> suggests they all have world's worst job or they've all been recruited 
> from chip-on-my-shoulder hall of fame.

Well, actually, that wasn't too bad either. One time, though, I came in 
through Boston instead of directly to BWI. When I got on the plane from 
Boston to BWI, there was a guy in front of me with a lobster in a 
cardboard box. So I asked him where he got the lobster and he told me, 
"There's a store in the airport where you can buy them. I'm taking it 
home for dinner." And the lobster said, "I've already had dinner -- take 
me to a movie."

That does indeed sound awfully similar.  Worth a shot to set timeout
to zero (0).

Two other things to try:

1) reinstall grub to your MBR

# grub-install --recheck /dev/hdb

2) run this script as root and post the contents of output.txt:

true ; (
set -x
fdisk -l
) >& output.txt

My proposed "solution" to the "laptop search avoidance without raising
suspicion" problem is to run the laptop to the point the battery is nearly
flat, and pack your AC in your checked luggage. That way, you look
cooperative, but there's nothing you can do to make the machine work after
it turns off halfway through booting. "Oh, sorry, I watched a movie on the
flight. The battery must be dead."

:D

Note that I've never tried this, but I suspect it would work well.

I don't have Ken's problem, but I do need Quickbooks and InDesign,
neither of which can be made to work in Wine. So I have Windows 2000
installed in Virtualbox. But it's a pain in the ass. I have to launch
Windows just to print an invoice for a customer. (No, GnuCash has no
inventory control.)

Ken's issue is even more annoying. The web is *the web*. It should not
require proprietary formats. *The web* makes me think of things like
equal rights and equal access and neutrality. I am wearing at this
moment a plastic bracelet from Mozilla that says "I support the open
web." (Got it at OSCON last summer.)

The internet and all its features should be accessible to all humanity
regardless of *whatever*. The internet is humanity's best hope for
peace and progress. Its openness must be held as sacrosanct as free
speech and freedom of religion. The law should recognize this.

Of course another alternative is to run vnc server on your Windows 
system and run the vnc viewer on your Linux system.  At least you would 
not have to reach up to the KVM.   Another alternative is to run synergy 
on both systems.  That way you can use one keyboard and mouse to control 
both systems.  I must run my companies Windows laptop at home once in a  
while and use synergy from my Linux home system to control both 
systems.  Just move the mouse from one to the other.  At work I have 
three LCD monitors, 2 for a Linux system and 1 for the laptop and I even 
leave the laptop open to use its screen for email.  It's a pain to have 
to use only the laptop screen at home.

saves one the trouble of emulating a copy of Windows.

BTW, How do you install Windows 2000 so that you don't have to
be an administrator to use say a USB thumb drive?  What updates
are people using for 2000 these days?  I wish there was an open
source program to go out and get all of the 2000 updates and
create a private mirror that Windows update can be pointed at.
Same thing for XP.

Here's an idea, pass a law for updates to proprietary consumer
software forcing it to be open source.  So what if someone
reverse engineers your software, they still have to get away
with large volume distributions or sales of their stolden
property to actually hurt your bottom line.  You can take
your chunk out of an attempt to sell your software illegally.
There is always the option of a hefty fine for abusers of
open source proprietary software.  Open sourcing the updates
would be a boon for the honest users of your software and
make it more popular.

On Wed, 20 Feb 2008 10:02:33 -0800
robinson-west user <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> dijo:

> BTW, How do you install Windows 2000 so that you don't have to
> be an administrator to use say a USB thumb drive?  What updates
> are people using for 2000 these days?  I wish there was an open
> source program to go out and get all of the 2000 updates and
> create a private mirror that Windows update can be pointed at.
> Same thing for XP.

I don't know how it works with VMware and other virtual machine
utilities, but with VirtualBox I have no problem mounting a USB stick.
I just launch VirtualBox, select (but don't launch) the Windows 2000
machine, insert the USB stick, wait a sec for it to automount in Linux,
then open a terminal to see where Linux mounted it (in my
case /media/disk). Then I go into Shared Folders for the Windows 2000
machine and add this as a shared folder. Whenever I launch Windows 2000
this folder will show up in Windows Explorer along with any other
shared folders. Actually, you don't even have to go to this much
trouble - if you know that your distro will always mount stuff
in /media you can just add /media as the shared folder and then any
removable drives will be accessible there.

As for othjer USB devices, mice and keyboards are just automatically
available, but I don't know about things like modems or wifi cards and
such because I've never had any of those to worry about. 

As for Windows updates, since VirtualBox automatically configured the
network for Windows 2000 as well as Linux I can just go anywhere on the
net from Windows 2000 the same as I can from Linux. So to get an update
for Windows 2000 I just went off to microsoft.com and told it to do an
express update over the net. A short while later it was updated to SP4.
I can also update any other program that can be updated over the net,
e.g., Sun Java, etc., and I can install anything over the net (flash,
realplayer, etc.).

I haven't found anything that doesn't work in Windows 2000 under
VirtualBox the same as it does in Linux.

-----Original Message-----
From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org
[mailto:plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org] On Behalf Of Jason Van Cleve
Sent: Thursday, February 14, 2008 3:54 PM
To: General Linux/UNIX discussion and help; civil and on-topic; John Jason
Jordan
Subject: Re: [PLUG] Advanced Topics February meeting

Wow, Access, really?  I wrote a tool for my QA team to read sets of data
into Oracle for analysis, because playing with GUI shapes in Access gets
really frustrating and hard to edit, and its manual SQL
syntax--particular those parentheses-ridden joins--is nigh impossible to
work with.  They love my loader tool, because it gives them a "normal"
SQL interface (not to mention the power of a real RDBMS), and once you
get used to that, you won't go back to using GUIs.

No, there's no such thing as standard SQL, but most systems will support
90% of what you need (Access not included), with only minor differences.
The problem is, with an enterprise app', where performance is critical,
you'll inevitably find yourself tweaking your queries in system-specific
ways:  here a proprietary annotation, there a restructuring of
subqueries, just because your DB likes it better that way.  And that's
where ORMs fall short:  they simply can't know in every case what's the
best way to write a query.  They'll produce something that works, but to
make it hum, you're going to have to write that SQL by hand.

So I recommend biting that bullet and learning the basics of SQL, along
with whatever little quirks your DB introduces.  Learning outer joins,
"group by" and "having", "exists" and "not exists", and a few other
tricks will give you worlds of power, and you'll find those skills
really do transfer from one system to another, including Oracle, Sybase,
MySQL and Postgres (but not Access!).

With that knowledge, you'll be in a good position to tweak your queries
for best performance on whatever system you're using (except, well, you
know).

--Jason

> Kexi and create queries in OOo.
> Ultimately I need the data merged into a Writer document, and 
> OOo will happily merge the Kexi data into a Writer document 
> based on a query in Base. Previously, when I used Access, OOo 
> could merge only on a table, so I had to create a dummy table 
> that I would insert data into, then empty after the merge. 
> Merging on a query is much simpler.

On Sun, 2008-02-10 at 21:11 -0800, Tim wrote:

> - No reason to use /boot nowadays IMO, since grub is great and doesn't 
>   have size limitations for finding kernels.

Is grub able to handle logical volumes? This claims it is able to in
1.95, but as current as Fedora is typically, it has only 0.97. Ah, STFW:
1.95 is the new, in-development GRUB 2; 0.97 is the current stable
"legacy" version.

My habits for servers is to have to partitions: one for /boot and one
LVM Physical Volume. When I have two drives, these are (MD) RAID1
volumes; when I have two large drives, I create several partitions as
RAID1 volumes and use them as PVs in LVM. This allows me to not use them
until necessary and keep them stopped, so they don't try to sync if I
have to reboot uncleanly.

Wil

partitions, /boot may be useful.  For me, I just consider all of my
system partitions expendable.  I keep any important data on separate
partitions that can be mirrored.  If I'm worried about losing configs
in /etc or something, I just back them up to the mirrored set.  You can
also do full system mirrors without using LVM at all, so grub can boot
that fine.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Feb 21 21:35:38 2008

Subject: [PLUG] Cold Boot attacks on RAM

DRAM typically retains data images for much longer than a typical
refresh interval.  This paper describes cold boot attacks that
can read RAMs and extract encryption keys for minutes or even hours
after shutdown.  

http://citp.princeton.edu/memory/

Hardware does the darnest things ...

I have rather serious doubts. Short retention time is a major
performance bottleneck for RAM, if retention time could be even
doubled without creating errors all chipsets would use it (and on some
motherboards you can adjust the read delays, if you make it even 20%
longer than the memory supports you can get hundreds of errors per
second). And we're still talking about nanoseconds, not seconds.

DRAM module... Let's just say that I think an alternative explanation
is more likely (and I don't mean it's fake, fake would imply
intentional falsification).

Subject: [PLUG] Cold Boot attacks on RAM

> I don't think it's fake. But I also am not convinced it's correct

> is more likely (and I don't mean it's fake, fake would imply

Well, did you read the paper at all?

There's a big difference between having a DRAM refresh interval that is
reliable enough to keep your system from crashing (i.e. incredibly high
reliability on every bit) and an interval which is long enough to keep
some significant percentage of data intact.  

Keep in mind that these guys were cooling the RAM to -50degC in order to
get the desired longevity.  That's not normal operating conditions.
Fortunately, if you'd like to test this method out yourself, just shoot
canned air at the RAM while the can is upside down.  It's not like the
methods require expensive equipment to test, which speaks to it's
authenticity.

Finally, a big portion of the paper was dedicated to algorithms for
recovering partially corrupt encryption keys through improvised error
correcting codes.  They're obviously not implying that all of their bits
are intact.

I can't say that I've tried it, but these are all reasons not to dismiss
it outright.

tim

> is more likely (and I don't mean it's fake, fake would imply
> intentional falsification).

Can I weigh in here, as a chip designer who has designed memories?
The explanation will get a little technical.

Dynamic memories store charge on a tiny little capacitor, accessed
through a tiny little MOSFET.  The capacitors leak, and the transistors
leak - but not a whole lot.  The amount of leakage is a function of
process, power supply, temperature, supply noise, and manufacturing
imperfections.  In particular, the leakage typically goes up because
of noise, high power supply, and "fast" processing variations.  It
doubles every 10 degrees Celsius.  And it is very sensitive to 
imperfections;  the weakest cell out of billions may leak as much
as 1000 times as much as the vast majority of typical cells.  

Since manufacturers don't want to throw away any more chips than
absolutely necessary, they design the average cells so that the
rare outliers with 1000 times as much leakage, 125C temperatures,
high noisy supplies, and worst case processing, will STILL retain
retain their value for the typical 2 *millisecond* refresh cycle.
Run the same chip at -55C (spray freeze) or -196C (liquid nitrogen),
with reduced supply noise and power voltage, and it will indeed
retain state in the vast majority of the bit cells for minutes to
hours.  Further, if a RAM cell stays powered up with the same bit
value for months at a time, it tends to "burn in" the value a tiny
little bit, and when powered up will often show that bit value
before it is overwritten.

This behavior can actually be pesky in static RAMs;  if one of
the transistors in the static latch of the cell is busted, then
the RAM will act like a long-lifetime dynamic RAM - it will retain
value for many seconds, making the defect difficult to find in a
quick RAM test (semiconductor testers cost about $200 per hour to
run, so long tests are expensive).  In the field, where static
RAM information is expected to survive without rewrite forever,
these effects will bite you.  

Yes, there are some mechanisms that fail if you slow the clock.
Synchronous DRAM (SDRAM) and the various DDR designs have clock
synchronizers called phase locked loops that get their knickers
in a twist if the system clock stops or is too slow.  That can
result in write errors.  But it won't rewrite the whole chip;
the vast majority of the bits are unaddressed and undisturbed.

So yes indeed, the vast majority of the cells in a Dynamic Random
Access Memory will retain value for a surprisingly long time.  The
decay mechanism the Princeton folk describe in their paper - a
long period of nearly perfect retention, followed by a relatively
short period of high decay, is exactly what you would expect
given the physics.  The temperature behavior is, too.  And the
memory will decay more slowly if the chip is powered down,
because fewer leakage paths are activated by stress and supply
noise, so an out-of-socket RAM will retain a pattern longer.

I can point you at papers and books on dynamic memory design and
testing if you want to slog through some heavy circuit theory.
Otherwise, you will have to take my word for it, or ask another
memory designer if still skeptical.  

If I can find the time, I hope to put together a presentation for
Advanced Topics titled "Bits Are a Myth", about many pesky ways
that digital electronic circuits are not always digital, the cheezy
noisy analog signals that your disk drive read head and cable modem
and wireless receiver sees, and new technologies like RAZR that
speed up logic to negative voltage and timing margins, then repeat
the calculations that fail.  It's an analog world out there, and
it will bite you on the ass with a tiny but nonzero probability.

Programmers that assume perfect computers, and do not incorporate
periodic consistency checks on data and program state, are not only
subjecting their users to security fragility and undiscovered program
flaws, but also to random analog unkindness and senseless acts of
indeterminate nonbeauty.  As we scale down the energy per computation
step, it will only get worse.  They taught self-correcting software
and hardware back when I was in school, when computers were less
reliable;  they should start doing that again.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Message-ID: <86wsoxuycv.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


In Common Lisp and Scheme you often find a reliable compatibility
between print and read for floating point values.  That is, for any
float you can print it to a string and then parse that string back
into the same float from an equivalence point of view.

   Printing Floating-Point Numbers Quickly and Accurately
   http://www.cs.indiana.edu/~burger/FP-Printing-PLDI96.pdf

   How to Read Floating Point Numbers Accurately
   ftp://ftp.ccs.neu.edu/pub/people/will/howtoread.ps

In Common Lisp there is a function that "decodes" a float into the
various integer parts (sign, mantissa and exponent) in such a way that
you can reconstruct the float from the integer components, thusly:

   [1]> (integer-decode-float 1.0)
   4503599627370496 ;
   -52 ;
   1

   [2]> (multiple-value-bind (m e s) 
          (integer-decode-float 1.0) 
            (* s m (expt 2.0 e)))
   1.0

Note, the second command is binding the multiple values to (m)antissa,
(e)xponent and (s)ign and then recomposing them by multiplication:
sign * mantissa * 2^exponent, and returning the same value you fed to
integer-decode-float (1.0 in this case, but it will work for any
floating point number).

I have an application in which a C program wants to pass floating
point values (doubles, as it happens) accurately over a network socket
to a lisp program without a lot of messy float printing and parsing.
Integers are so much nicer.

So, I tried to write an equivalent to integer-decode-float in C.  I
did.  It worked.  For a while.  Maybe my C is rusty.  Here it is in
testable isolation:

   #include <stdio.h>
   #include <stdlib.h>

   struct decoded_float {
     unsigned sign;
     signed exponent;
     unsigned long long mantissa;
   };

   struct decoded_float*
   decode_float(double value) 
   {
     static static decoded_float d;

     unsigned long long *p,v;

     p = (unsigned long long *) &value;
     v = *p;

     d.sign = v >> 63;
     d.exponent = v << 1 >> 53;
     d.mantissa = v << 12 >> 12;

     printf("s e m = %u %d %llu\n",d.sign,d.exponent,d.mantissa);

     if(d.exponent)
       d.mantissa |= (unsigned long long) 1 << 52;

     printf("s e m = %u %d %llu\n",d.sign,d.exponent,d.mantissa);

     d.exponent -= 1023 + 52;

     printf("s e m = %u %d %llu\n",d.sign,d.exponent,d.mantissa);

     return &d;
   }

   int 
   main(int argc, char** argv) 
   {
     double v = atof(argv[1]);
     struct decoded_float* f = decode_float(v);  

     printf("s e m = %u %d %llu\n",f->sign,f->exponent,f->mantissa);

     return 0;
   }        

[Note: s here is 0 or 1 rather than -1 or +1 in the lisp equivalent
... that's okay, I can deal with that.]

Again, this seemed to be working fine.  Then, after about 4 years I
wanted to run this stuff again and I noticed some errors, particularly
when I ramped up the optimization.  

   $ for i in $(seq 0 6) ; do echo Using -O$i ; gcc -O$i decoded-float.c -o decoded-float ; ./decoded-float 1.0 ; done
   Using -O0
   s e m = 0 1023 0
   s e m = 0 1023 4503599627370496
   s e m = 0 -52 4503599627370496
   s e m = 0 -52 4503599627370496
   Using -O1
   s e m = 0 1023 0
   s e m = 0 1023 4503599627370496
   s e m = 0 -52 4503599627370496
   s e m = 0 -52 4503599627370496
   Using -O2
   s e m = 1 894 1618429576478720
   s e m = 1 894 6122029203849216
   s e m = 1 -181 6122029203849216
   s e m = 1 -181 6122029203849216
   Using -O3
   s e m = 0 128 1294403149217721
   s e m = 0 128 5798002776588217
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776588217
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776588217
   Using -O4
   s e m = 0 128 1294403149725625
   s e m = 0 128 5798002777096121
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002777096121
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002777096121
   Using -O5
   s e m = 0 128 1294403149512633
   s e m = 0 128 5798002776883129
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776883129
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776883129
   Using -O6
   s e m = 0 128 1294403149254585
   s e m = 0 128 5798002776625081
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776625081
   s e m = 0 -947 5798002776625081

Note, with -O0 and -O1, I get the same decodes as the Lisp
integer-decode-float.  In -O2 it goes wrong and it goes wrong
differently in -O3 and above.

   $ gcc --version
   gcc (GCC) 4.2.3 (Debian 4.2.3-1)

Any clues what I am doing wrong?

seniorr at aracnet.com

My guess is that you are doing nothing wrong, but you're seeing
differences caused by precision difference of x86 floating-point
registers and x86 double word (80 bits vs 64). Lisp may do what in GCC
is called "float-store" (you can compile with -ffloat-store to test
the effect). Or Lisp may change the FPU control to use 64-bit
registers, it's a system call, you can do it in your program too:

    #include <fpu_control.h>
    unsigned short cw = 0;
    _FPU_GETCW(cw); // get the FPU control word
    cw &= ~_FPU_EXTENDED; // mask out '80-bit' register precision
    cw |=  _FPU_DOUBLE;   // mask in  IEEE '64-bit' register precision
    _FPU_SETCW(cw); // set the FPU control word

-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

long with -O2.

This feature is called Session Restore. The information to restore the
session is kept in ~/.mozilla/firefox/?????.default/sessionstore.js
I just tried tried the following experiment. I killed firefox on my desktop
and on my laptop. I then backed up sessionstore.js on my laptop and
overwrote it with sessionstore.js from the desktop and restarted firefox.
Now I had most of my windows from the desktop start right up. Some
complained about  expired certificates but I bet you could solve that by
copying over some certificate database also.

three. 

There has to be a way to do this. There just has to be. Are there any

Subject: [PLUG] HSQLDB string operations
In-Reply-To: <20080223172757.c7914c93.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080223172757.c7914c93.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <47C0CB22.2000705@bruce4.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I need to sort a query baased on the rightmost three characters of a
> field using HSQLDB, which is what OOo Base uses. The total number of
> characters in this field varies from one record to the next, so I can't
> just tell it to ignore the leftmost X number of characters. I went here:
>
> http://hsqldb.org/web/hsqlDocsFrame.html
>
> And found a list of built in operations, but I can't find a right-string
> (length) type operator. I found a length operator, and a substring
> operator that works from the left for len characters, but no right
> (length) operator.
>
> The problem is that the query I created in the GUI is sorting the data
> on the entire contents of the field, where I need it sorted only on the
> final three characters in the field, ignoring everything before the last
> three characters. Base lets me edit the query in SQL, but I need to
> figure out how to tell it to ignore all characters before the last
> three. 
>
> There has to be a way to do this. There just has to be. Are there any
> HSQLDB experts in here?
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>   

John,

You'll have to build a formula for this and use the "mid" function (the
substring operator). What you want is the starting position to be
(length-3) and the number of characters to be 3. I don't know the exact
format, but hopefully you can figure it out from that.

Be warned that this is VERY performance intensive and could be time
consuming depending on the number of records you're searching.

Tim

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Sat Feb 23 23:34:30 2008
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 23 Feb 2008 23:34:30 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MetroFi News
In-Reply-To: <20080223205700.da184fc0.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <1203827304.6325.64.camel@UbuntuGG>
	<20080223205700.da184fc0.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <86skzirent.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "John" == John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> writes:

>> "After installing 590 functional wireless access points (APs) in
>> Portland, MetroFi has asked the city to provide $9 million in
>> public funds to finish the job with an additional 2,000-or-so APs."

John> Interesting news.

It is actually somewhat old news.  There was an article back on
February 1 in the Portland Business Journal with the same information,
and indeed the only thing new in the BJ article was the amount that
MetroFi was asking ($3 to $9 million, depending on the "solution").
MetroFi announced they were stopping construction back in October.
They still have about a hundred devices sitting on poles not doing
anything.

The new news was OPB's interview with MetroFi CEO Chuck Haas late last
week, in which it sounds slightly like Chuck had just been awoken from
a nap:

  http://www.unwirepdx-watch.org/files/haas-interview.mp3

linked from here with a little commentary from yours truly:

  http://unwirepdx-watch.org/index.php/2008/02/21/interview-of-chuck-haas-on-opb-this-morning/

John> The city uses MetroFi for everything, up to and including
John> parking meters. How will parking meters be able to access your
John> debit card account if the wireless service goes away?

Parking meters were part of the original pitch, but it turns out the
City never did use wifi for that.  They are still using cellphone
technology, though they have changed the way they process transactions
recently (they used to batch them up until the end of the day, which
cost the city more in bank fees due to "card-not-present", but cost
them less in air-time and the customer less in transaction time.  now
they do the transaction while you wait).  Apparently, the vendor of
the parking meter kiosk thingies said it would cost too much to switch
to wifi.

Furthermore, according to Logan Kleier (the city project manager), the
city is not relying on MetroFi much in any other way.  Something like
20-30 workers use it now (even though it covers only a small fraction
of the city) and one office has a fixed-wireless service for something
like $300/month.  The BJ article said that the City could get higher
bandwidth than they have now into police cars for "a fraction" of the
$9 million MetroFi wants by buying the service through Verizon.

So, to the contrary, the City is *not* using MetroFi for everything,
or even much of anything.

John> I have a hunch that MetroFi will get a financial shot in the arm
John> from the city. Grant, loan, or whatever, the only other choice
John> the city has is to embrace another provider.

I don't think that is going to happen.  The city has been asked by
MetroFi several times to do this and so far the city has had the
fortitude to say: "heh, no thanks".  As far as I can tell, the City
has been in a somewhat passive "please fulfill your contract
obligations" mode for a while now, but hasn't been willing to actively
tell MetroFi to take down their gear and get out of town.

If MetroFi gets a shot in the arm from anywhere, my guess is it comes
from Microsoft, which is already involved.

russell at unwirepdx-watch.org
http://www.unwirepdx-watch.org

Dear Rogan,

I am a linux god.  And you deified me.

After your last email, I almost sent the email way at the bottom of
this email  to the PLUG and you.  I was so sick and tired of trying to
compile emacs from source.  I decided to leave the "compiling from
source emacs" problem alone for awhile.  Then I read you last email
that you sent me yet again, combined it with what I read in the text
file:

~/src/emacs-22.1/etc/PROBLEMS

I wound up doing :

apt-get install libncurses5-dev

Then I did

./configure -prefix=/opt

and

make

and furthermore

make install

and finally

 /opt/bin/emacs

and glory of glories, my emacs editor came up!  I have been having so
much angst over this for the longest of times.  I've been veritably
obsessed by it.

The following are my motivations and problems viz a viz linux.

If I'm going to use some crazy free non-mainstream operating system, I
might as well use the craziest, most free version of all.  I settled
on Debian.

Since I'm using such a hard operating system, I might as well learn to
use the command line, because "Real Men Do It From The Command Line".

Since I'm engaging in self-flagellation, I might as well add the
hardest version of text editors to the mix, i.e emacs.

I feel the version of emacs that you get when you do apt-get install
emacs, is inadequate  ( I actually want to use more colorful language
of what I think of Debian and emacs at this juncture! ) I decided, and
was influenced, to get a more modern version of emacs.

Because I wanted a modern version of emacs, I decided I would do it
with source code.

When I finally figured out how to compile from source, I succeeded
with compiling emacs from source, and when I went to invoke emacs,
i.e.

/opt/bin/emacs

I got the stupid frustrating, enraging,

emacs: Cannot open termcap database file

At this point, I gave up trying to get emacs to work.  When Rogan
Creswick said do:

apt-get install libncurses5-dev

and then told me to reconfigure, et. al.

emacs finally worked!

I did not realize that the whole configuration process does not update
your PATH.  I think some of my Windows programs do that.

I did not realize that I had to use a fully qualified path name to
invoke the program if my PATH wasn't updated.

I did not realize what commands in the configuration process could be
done as a regular user, and what had to be done as root.

I wasn't sure how to become root.

A lot of unnerving, fingernails scratching on the cardboard, major
little obstacles waylaid me on my "Royal Road to learning emacs"

Now...I guess I have to learn to use the darn editor.  Uh...Oh...

So, let me wax eloquently viz a viz Rogan Creswick and getting emacs
to work.  I couldn't have done it without him, and more generally, I
couldn't have done it without PLUG!  May my testimonial bear good
fruit.

Sincerely,
Victor Soich





The following is an email that I almost sent to PLUG and Rogan
Creswick.  I'm glad I didn't because I finally got emacs to work from
the command line.


Dear PLUG,

I am endeavoring to compile emacs from source.  I believe I have
successfully did the configure, make, make install stuff.  The
following is what is on my system:



[vsoich at c-24-22-47-214 /opt/bin]$ ls

emacs: Cannot open termcap database file
[vsoich at c-24-22-47-214 /opt/bin]$


The system cannot open termcap database file.


In the text file:

~/src/emacs-22.1/etc/PROBLEMS

It says:


** Emacs does not start, complaining that it cannot open termcap database file.

If your system uses Terminfo rather than termcap (most modern
systems do), this could happen if the proper version of
ncurses is not visible to the Emacs configure script (i.e. it
cannot be found along the usual path the linker looks for
libraries). It can happen because your version of ncurses is
obsolete, or is available only in form of binaries.

The solution is to install an up-to-date version of ncurses in
the developer's form (header files, static libraries and
symbolic links); in some GNU/Linux distributions (e.g. Debian)
it constitutes a separate package.



I don't know what it means to install an up-to date version of ncurses
in the developer's form ( header files, static libaries and symbolic
links)

I can do apt-caches search, and apt-get install, but I don't know what
to search for or install specifically.  Once I do install the
appropriate stuff, do I need to do configure, make, make install, on
my emacs source code again?

Sincerely,
Victor Soich

From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sun, 24 Feb 2008 12:49:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] HSQLDB string operations
In-Reply-To: <20080224022007.GA17875@patch.com>

On Sat, 23 Feb 2008 18:20:10 -0800
Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > I need to sort a query baased on the rightmost three characters of a
> > field using HSQLDB, which is what OOo Base uses. 
> 
> What's wrong with:
> 
> RIGHT(s,count)
> 
>     returns the rightmost count of characters of s
> 
> ??
> 
> RIGHT(field,3)...

Thanks to you and Tim for the suggestions. It turns out that I was trying to create the query in OOo Base (which uses HSQL) but run it on a Kexi (SQLite3) database to which the Base database was connected. Therefore, I needed the SQL to be in SQLite3, not HSQL. Unfortunately, SQLite3 can't seem to do it either. Plus SQLite3 has nowhere near the capabilities of HSQL.

Therefore, I spent several hours and finally got the Kexi data in to an OOo Base database. (You really do not want the blow-by-blow process, because it was not pretty. Believe it or not, Base cannot import anything, not even a CSV file. Lots of imprecations.)

So now that I have the data in Base I am again trying to sort the field based on the rightmost three characters of the selection string. Note that I need to sort on the rightmost three characters of the *selection string*, not the rightmost three characters of the *field*. Base will let me sort on the field, but in design view (the GUI) there seems to be no way to specify an Expression to sort on. Sorting on the field gives me this SQL:

SELECT * FROM "MainBank" WHERE ( ( "Selections" LIKE '%LFc-___%' ) ) ORDER BY "Selections" ASC

I need to change that ORDER BY so it reflects the rightmost three characters of LIKE '%LFc___%'. I have tried several things but all I get is error messages. I think I need a WHERE nested inside somehow, something like:

SELECT * FROM "MainBank" WHERE ( ( "Selections" LIKE '%LFc-___%' ) ) ORDER BY "Selections" WHERE ( RIGHT,3 ( LIKE '%___%' ) )  ASC

But that doesn't work, nor do any of my other attempts. Either my logic is wrong or I have misplaced parentheses, quote marks, or something.

Suggestions to fix the syntax are welcome.

Subject: [PLUG] HSQLDB string operations
In-Reply-To: <20080224124920.646dffd6.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Sun, 24 Feb 2008 12:49:20 -0800
John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> dijo:

> So now that I have the data in Base I am again trying to sort the field based on the rightmost three characters of the selection string. Note that I need to sort on the rightmost three characters of the *selection string*, not the rightmost three characters of the *field*. Base will let me sort on the field, but in design view (the GUI) there seems to be no way to specify an Expression to sort on. Sorting on the field gives me this SQL:
> 
> SELECT * FROM "MainBank" WHERE ( ( "Selections" LIKE '%LFc-___%' ) ) ORDER BY "Selections" ASC
> 
> I need to change that ORDER BY so it reflects the rightmost three characters of LIKE '%LFc___%'. I have tried several things but all I get is error messages. I think I need a WHERE nested inside somehow, something like:
> 
> SELECT * FROM "MainBank" WHERE ( ( "Selections" LIKE '%LFc-___%' ) ) ORDER BY "Selections" WHERE ( RIGHT,3 ( LIKE '%___%' ) )  ASC
> 
> But that doesn't work, nor do any of my other attempts. Either my logic is wrong or I have misplaced parentheses, quote marks, or something.

Never mind. I got it sorted finally:

SELECT * FROM "MainBank" 
WHERE ( ( "Selections" LIKE '%LFc-___%' ) ) 
ORDER BY SUBSTR( "Selections", LOCATE(  'LFc-', "Selections")  + 4, 3) ASC

On Sun, Feb 24, 2008 at 03:21:35PM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
> 
> To gain user access, do I source /etc/fstab or just log out and back in as
> a user?

Add 'user' to the options field of your fstab entry. For example:

  /dev/sda   /mnt/meizu  vfat  user,noauto  0  0

Aren't you an XFCE user? It should be automounting USB mass storage
devices for you. Plug in your player and see if an entry shows up in
the XFCE file browser's sidebar. If it doesn't, see if you've got HAL
installed.

-- 
Paul

On Sun, Feb 24, 2008 at 03:39:00PM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
 
> > Add 'user' to the options field of your fstab entry. For example:
> 
> It's there. Apparently not immediately picked up.

The 'user' option allows regular users to mount and unmount file
systems. If a file system was mounted by root, it's likely that it
won't be accessible to regular users (depending on the permissions
set).

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sun Feb 24 18:17:09 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

Message-ID: <200802241817.09910.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Victor Soich
# on Sunday 24 February 2008 16:14:

>If I open up the terminal, will all the commands in the Easy Linux
>Commands work exactly the same?

"Yes, but no".  The mac has a BSD userland, so ls/find/rm are 
implemented differently.  In general, they work about the same, but 
have a less generous options-parsing scheme (e.g. the directory name is 
not optional with `find` and option flags can almost never go after the 
arguments.)  You may also find gnu features missing in tail, head, etc.

--Eric

software:  a hypothetical exercise which happens to compile.

>

Mac OS X has Darwin as its UNIX core, which is BSD-ish.
When you go back far enough it derives from NeXTSTEP UNIX, which was Steve
Job's other project back in the 90s, and that was BSD based.
You can still see some of the NeXT lineage in the OS X file manager among
other places, if you switch the file view to three column mode, this is
exactly how NeXTSTEP did it.

So to boil it down, when you open a terminal on OS X, it will be BSD-ish
rather than System V-ish (like most current Linux distros).
Most of the "beginner" Linux commands should work more or less (pun
intended).
For example you would use "ps aux" instead of "ps -ef".

Here are some good resources:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/macxtigerunix/
http://www.osxfaq.com/Tutorials/LearningCenter/





-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight


From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Sun Feb 24 18:22:50 2008
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Sun, 24 Feb 2008 18:22:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Relationship between Mac and Linux
References: <498756ed0802241614x5c352198j25ecde7f8a687a75@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86fxvhn5ad.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Victor" == Victor Soich <vsoich at gmail.com> writes:

Victor> If I open up the terminal, will all the commands in the Easy Linux
Victor> Commands work exactly the same.  Remember, these are beginner linux
Victor> commands.  Or is there some gotcha that I should be aware of?

The Unix of OSX is more Unix than Linux is[1].  All the same basic commands
you're familiar with should be there in a Terminal window... "ls", "cat",
"grep", etc.  Even emacs, and if it's installed, gcc.  Perl is already there,
and you can build most open source tools directly from the net.  You should be
right at home.  You can even run X11 programs if they've installed the
optional X11 package.

[1] OSX Leopard has actually been OpenGroup-certified as "unix(tm)".
    See <http://www.apple.com/macosx/technology/unix.html>.  Linux
    has not met that qualification that I'm aware of.

On Sun, 24 Feb 2008, Matt McKenzie wrote:

>> If I open up the terminal, will all the commands in the Easy Linux 
>> Commands work exactly the same.  Remember, these are beginner linux 
>> commands.  Or is there some gotcha that I should be aware of?
>
> Mac OS X has Darwin as its UNIX core, which is BSD-ish. When you go 
> back far enough it derives from NeXTSTEP UNIX, which was Steve Job's 
> other project back in the 90s, and that was BSD based. You can still 
> see some of the NeXT lineage in the OS X file manager among other 
> places, if you switch the file view to three column mode, this is 
> exactly how NeXTSTEP did it.
>
> So to boil it down, when you open a terminal on OS X, it will be 
> BSD-ish rather than System V-ish (like most current Linux distros). 
> Most of the "beginner" Linux commands should work more or less (pun 
> intended). For example you would use "ps aux" instead of "ps -ef".

The newer BSD utils in Leopard (10.5) behave more GNU-like than those 
in Tiger (10.4). In fact, the very example you gave is one of the 
changes; I know, because I like "ps -ef" better than "ps aux" for some 
things.

In Tiger, "ps -ef" fails, but it will succeed in Leopard.

Furthermore, while ps/ls and the like are from the BSD side of things, 
the GNU version of several utilites are there, including tar, grep, 
m4, and make.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com


From ron at braithwaites.net  Sun Feb 24 21:19:30 2008
From: ron at braithwaites.net (Ron Braithwaite)
Date: Sun, 24 Feb 2008 21:19:30 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Relationship between Mac and Linux

On Feb 24, 2008, at 6:22 PM, Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
<snip>
> you can build most open source tools directly from the net.

That is, if you have installed Xtools off the installer CD. Xtools is  
a very, very nice development environment, but it is not installed by  
default.

> You can even run X11 programs if they've installed the optional X11  
> package.

Actually, X11 is now installed by default under OSX 10.5 Leopard.

Incidentally, if he hasn't updated to 10.5.2, do so for him. There are  
a metric butt-load of fixes in that version with tons of regression  
testing, so almost all of the complaints folks have had about 10.5  
have been fixed.

*AFTER* you have installed Xtools, you can get full-blown access to  
open source packages, with http://www.macports.org/install.php This  
is  a command line version of the BSD port utility (equivalent to  
apt). Read the man page and http://trac.macports.org/projects/macports/wiki/FAQ 
  for more details.

Some of us lightweights have grown fond of GUIs (I started with  
version 5, so I don't have any excuse except I'm lazy), so we also  
load Pallet, which is still in development, but is really promising.  
You can get Pallet by running:

sudo port install pallet

Finally, http://www.apple.com/downloads/macosx/unix_open_source/portauthority.html 
  gets you a copy of Port Authority, which is a $20 shareware software  
front end equivalent to Synaptic. It's what I use while Pallet gets  
stable. In fact, I'll probably run it after Pallet is stable, since I  
don't mind giving other developers a few bucks when they make my life  
easier.

At any rate, OSX Leopard is the real deal, as far as Unix is  
concerned. And there is *lots* of Free/Libre Open Source Software  
available for it.

Good to see you back in the game, Victor.

-Ron

From m0gely at telestream.com  Mon Feb 25 08:30:31 2008

Date: Mon, 25 Feb 2008 08:30:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cold Boot attacks on RAM
In-Reply-To: <20080222075807.GB14819@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20080222053538.GA14673@gate.kl-ic.com>	<a4fbdc7b0802212152l53c4f5eo86e10d14c6a30e7@mail.gmail.com>	<fe37588d0802212205v30566ea9u5081fb9ab2955954@mail.gmail.com>	<a4fbdc7b0802212219x31f39c50vffa6460002c6d209@mail.gmail.com>
	<20080222075807.GB14819@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <47C2ED27.4060104@telestream.com>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> Can I weigh in here, as a chip designer who has designed memories?
> The explanation will get a little technical.

So, just having installed TrueCrypt on my spare laptop and encrypted the 
system drive, I saw this video a few days ago and was impressed by the 
simplicity of it all. I'm sure the TC guys (et al) are working on 
solutions, but do you think that in situations like this, when the key 
is entered at boot, it's then passed onto the disk where it is now 
readable? Then an operation can be done to zero out either the part of 
memory that contained the key, or all the memory if necessary before 
proceeding to boot the OS? I don't know what kind of performance 
penalties would be incurred though, or is such an idea is even good at all.

It was interesting seeing the picture example, as it reminded me of 
resetting my Zaurus. The contents on the display last, would fade out 
like that on a reset.

- m0gely


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Feb 25 09:24:21 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 25 Feb 2008 09:24:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cold Boot attacks on RAM
In-Reply-To: <47C2ED27.4060104@telestream.com>
References: <20080222053538.GA14673@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<a4fbdc7b0802212152l53c4f5eo86e10d14c6a30e7@mail.gmail.com>
	<fe37588d0802212205v30566ea9u5081fb9ab2955954@mail.gmail.com>
	<a4fbdc7b0802212219x31f39c50vffa6460002c6d209@mail.gmail.com>
	<20080222075807.GB14819@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<47C2ED27.4060104@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <20080225172421.GA24725@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Feb 25, 2008 at 08:30:31AM -0800, m0gely wrote:
> 
> So, just having installed TrueCrypt on my spare laptop and encrypted the 
> system drive, I saw this video a few days ago and was impressed by the 
> simplicity of it all. I'm sure the TC guys (et al) are working on 
> solutions, but do you think that in situations like this, when the key 
> is entered at boot, it's then passed onto the disk where it is now 
> readable? Then an operation can be done to zero out either the part of 
> memory that contained the key, or all the memory if necessary before 
> proceeding to boot the OS? I don't know what kind of performance 
> penalties would be incurred though, or is such an idea is even good at all.

The key stays in RAM. In fact, for most of these programs the key is stored
in a partially precomputed form, such as "key schedules for block ciphers"
to quote the paper.  These precomputations greatly minimize the delay
associated with decrypting the hard drive,  however, they create a lot
of redundancy, which makes key recovery after partial decay much easier. 
Without the precomputation, disk access would be pig slow.

A BIOS boot sequence that clears the memory (such as a memory check)
provides partial protection.  At least, that occurs before the boot
loader pulls who-knows-what-program from who-knows-what boot device.
That means the bad guys have to take the time to disable the RAM check,
or take the time to open the laptop in preparation for removing the 
memory card.  

Programs like TrueCrypt are still valuable;  if your laptop is stolen,
the thief is not a spy 99% of the time, and won't have tools necessary
to recover the key.  If the thief sells your laptop to a spy, the
memory will have been long since erased and written over.  You can't
expect perfect security with any program or hardware;  but for a given
expenditure in effort and performance, you can reduce the risk of loss.

Storing data /anywhere/ has risks and costs associated with it.  Taking
that data outside of a controlled environment greatly adds to the risks.
Most sensitive data should never go anywhere near a laptop.

I assume my laptop will be stolen, someday.  I expect that the bad guys
will see the "weird" Linux on it and realize that it will have to be
rewritten with Windows before it can be sold to a fence.  Chances are,
the laptop will be trashed and discarded or overwritten.  So, my risk
is not zero, but smaller than it would be if I was running a more
popular OS.

> It was interesting seeing the picture example, as it reminded me of 
> resetting my Zaurus. The contents on the display last, would fade out 
> like that on a reset.

That wasn't the RAM you were seeing, but the effect is similar.  The
voltages that drive the liquid crystals in an LCD display are also
stored on pixel capacitors between refreshes, and they decay in a
similar fashion.  So the fading you saw on your Zaurus is quite a good
analogy.  Cooling the display would slow the decay, although liquid
crystals are also temperature sensitive, so the slow decay would be
affecting a bad picture.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

On Sun, Feb 24, 2008 at 4:14 PM, Victor Soich <vsoich at gmail.com> wrote:
>
>  If I open up the terminal, will all the commands in the Easy Linux
>  Commands work exactly the same.  Remember, these are beginner linux
>  commands.  Or is there some gotcha that I should be aware of?

One thing that cause me *many* problems is that the file system in OS
X is *NOT* case-sensitive, but it does preserve case (and the
auto-completion *IS* case-sensitive).  This is one of the main
reason's I don't use a mac any more -- the first time I realized that
Documents and documents were the same folder was when I obliterated
one with the other :(.  (This was a while ago -- I don't expect that
the filesystem is suddenly case-sensitive, but perhaps the tools make
it more obvious now.)

You can make a volume case sensitive.  You just have to format it as UFS,
not the HFS+ format.  (Some packages will not build under OSX unless you
build on such a partition.)

My big problem with the Mac (besides the single button mouse) is the
proprietary nature of the OS.  Apple still acts like they own you.  The
patents on their interface alone are enough to make me not want to use
their OS.  Yes it has a Unix core.  But it also has a proprietary mindset
everywhere you (next)step.

> build on such a partition.)

Unfortunately the mac will not boot off of a case-sensitive partition 
nor will many mac-native applications install to a case sensitive partition.

--Brent

# from Rogan Creswick
# on Monday 25 February 2008 10:53:

>One thing that cause me *many* problems is that the file system in OS
>X is *NOT* case-sensitive, but it does preserve case (and the
>auto-completion *IS* case-sensitive).

Oh, I forgot about that "fun".  They should have just put that in the 
GUI layer, but instead they put it all the way down at the filesystem.  
Unfortunately, they forgot to break all of the shell tools to match.  
So, `find` doesn't play the same game as `ls` and `rm`.  IIRC, they 
didn't deal with anything besides ASCII case either -- so utf8 case 
*is* sensitive (duh, 65==97 but 256!=257.)

> One thing that cause me *many* problems is that the file system in OS
> X is *NOT* case-sensitive, but it does preserve case (and the
> auto-completion *IS* case-sensitive).  This is one of the main
> reason's I don't use a mac any more -- the first time I realized that

Actually, under OSX 10.5, you have the option of volumes being HFS+

workstations, simply because some older applications (e.g., Microsoft  
Office 2004) get confused.

about needing case sensitive volumes for build.  I just set up a seperate

problem I ran into was that some autoconf versions did not recognize
"darwin" and needed to be upgraded.  I even had Squid and Hylafax built
and running under 10.3.4.  Not hard once you ditch Fink and build your own
tools.

From michael at jamhome.us  Mon Feb 25 14:33:16 2008
From: michael at jamhome.us (Michael Rasmussen)

Subject: [PLUG] HSQLDB string operations
Message-ID: <26752.170.135.241.46.1203978796.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>

On Sun, Feb 24, 2008 at 12:49:20PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > > I need to sort a query baased on the rightmost three characters of a
> > > field using HSQLDB, which is what OOo Base uses.
> >
> > What's wrong with:
> >
> > RIGHT(s,count)
> >
> >     returns the rightmost count of characters of s
> >
> > ??
> >
> > RIGHT(field,3)...
>
> Thanks to you and Tim for the suggestions. It turns out that I was
trying to create the query in OOo Base (which uses HSQL) but run it on a
Kexi (SQLite3) database to which the Base database was connected.
Therefore, I needed the SQL to be in SQLite3, not HSQL. Unfortunately,
SQLite3 can't seem to do it either. Plus SQLite3 has nowhere near the
capabilities of HSQL.

Are you sure?  Had I known you were asking about sqlite I would have written:

What's wrong with:

  substr(s,-3)

substr(X,Y,Z)
substr(X,Y)
  Return a substring of input string X that begins with the Y-th
  character and which is Z characters long. If Z is omitted then all
  character through the end of the string are returned. The left-most
  character of X is number 1. If Y is negative the the first character
  of the substring is found by counting from the right rather than the
  left. If X is string then characters indices refer to actual UTF-8
  characters. If X is a BLOB then the indices refer to bytes.

> Are you sure?  Had I known you were asking about sqlite I would have written:

> What's wrong with:

>   substr(s,-3)

> substr(X,Y,Z)
> substr(X,Y)
>   Return a substring of input string X that begins with the Y-th
>   character and which is Z characters long. If Z is omitted then all
>   character through the end of the string are returned. The left-most
>   character of X is number 1. If Y is negative the the first character
>   of the substring is found by counting from the right rather than the
>   left. If X is string then characters indices refer to actual UTF-8
>   characters. If X is a BLOB then the indices refer to bytes.

More that I failed to say: Yes, I was trying it in SQLite3, but the
data is in Kexi. Kexi uses SQLite3, but only a small portion of the
SQLKIte3 features have been implemented, and they don't yet include
substr. Even though I am writing the query in an OOo base database that
is connected to the Kexi database, the query language I use in Base has
to be SQLite3.

I solved the problem by moving the data to Base where I can use HSQL
queries. The Base GUI does not implement all features of HSQL yet, but
all HSQL features can be used if you write the statement manually. And
you can sort of do it in the GUI, you just have to write the substr
part in the box where you would normally put a field.

Base is pig slow compared to Kexi, but I can't wait for Kexi to
implement the features that I need. Someday maybe Kexi will mature to
the point where it has the features I need and I can move back.
